media</span></span></a></div>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Atlantic_Ocean_to_Africa.ogv" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>This video was taken by the crew of <a href="/wiki/Expedition_29" title="Expedition 29">Expedition 29</a> on board the <a href="/wiki/International_Space_Station" title="International Space Station">ISS</a>. The pass starts from just northeast of the island of <a href="/wiki/Newfoundland_(island)" title="Newfoundland (island)">Newfoundland</a> over the North Atlantic Ocean to central Africa, over <a href="/wiki/South_Sudan" title="South Sudan">South Sudan</a>.</div></div></div>\n<p>The <b>Atlantic Ocean</b> is the second largest of the world\'s <a href="/wiki/Ocean" title="Ocean">oceans</a>, with an area of about 106,460,000 square kilometers (41,100,000 square miles).<sup id="cite_ref-NOAA-how-big_2-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NOAA-how-big-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Atlantic_Ocean_-_Britannica_3-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Atlantic_Ocean_-_Britannica-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> It covers approximately 20 percent of the <a href="/wiki/Earth%27s_surface" class="mw-redirect" title="Earth&#39;s surface">Earth\'s surface</a> and about 29 percent of its water surface area. It separates the "<a href="/wiki/Old_World" title="Old World">Old World</a>" from the "<a href="/wiki/New_World" title="New World">New World</a>".\n</p><p>The Atlantic Ocean occupies an elongated, S-shaped basin extending longitudinally between <a href="/wiki/Europe" title="Europe">Europe</a> and <a href="/wiki/Africa" title="Africa">Africa</a> to the east, and the <a href="/wiki/Americas" title="Americas">Americas</a> to the west. As one component of the interconnected <a href="/wiki/Global_ocean" class="mw-redirect" title="Global ocean">global ocean</a>, it is connected in the north to the <a href="/wiki/Arctic_Ocean" title="Arctic Ocean">Arctic Ocean</a>, to the <a href="/wiki/Pacific_Ocean" title="Pacific Ocean">Pacific Ocean</a> in the southwest, the <a href="/wiki/Indian_Ocean" title="Indian Ocean">Indian Ocean</a> in the southeast, and the <a href="/wiki/Southern_Ocean" title="Southern Ocean">Southern Ocean</a> in the south (other definitions describe the Atlantic as extending southward to <a href="/wiki/Antarctica" title="Antarctica">Antarctica</a>). The <a href="/wiki/Equatorial_Counter_Current" title="Equatorial Counter Current">Equatorial Counter Current</a> subdivides it into the <b>North Atlantic Ocean</b> and the <b>South Atlantic Ocean</b> at about <a href="/wiki/8th_parallel_north" title="8th parallel north">8\xc2\xb0N</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-USN-2001_5-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-USN-2001-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Scientific explorations of the Atlantic include the <a href="/wiki/Challenger_expedition" title="Challenger expedition">Challenger expedition</a>, the <a href="/wiki/German_Meteor_expedition" title="German Meteor expedition">German Meteor expedition</a>, <a href="/wiki/Columbia_University" title="Columbia University">Columbia University</a>\'s <a href="/wiki/Lamont-Doherty_Earth_Observatory" class="mw-redirect" title="Lamont-Doherty Earth Observatory">Lamont-Doherty Earth Observatory</a> and the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Navy_Hydrographic_Office" class="mw-redirect" title="United States Navy Hydrographic Office">United States Navy Hydrographic Office</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-USN-2001_5-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-USN-2001-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div id="toc" class="toc"><input type="checkbox" role="button" id="toctogglecheckbox" class="toctogglecheckbox" style="display:none" /><div class="toctitle" lang="en" dir="ltr"><h2>Contents</h2><span class="toctogglespan"><label class="toctogglelabel" for="toctogglecheckbox"></label></span></div>\n<ul>\n<li floor</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-6"><a href="#Water_characteristics"><span class="tocnumber">4</span> <span class="toctext">Water characteristics</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-7"><a href="#Salinity"><span class="tocnumber">4.1</span> <span class="toctext">Salinity</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-8"><a href="#Water_masses"><span class="tocnumber">4.2</span> <span class="toctext">Water masses</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-9"><a href="#Gyres"><span Sea</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-11"><a href="#Climate"><span class="tocnumber">5</span> <span class="toctext">Climate</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 href="#Plate_tectonics"><span class="tocnumber">6</span> <span class="toctext">Plate tectonics</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-14"><a href="#Central_Atlantic"><span class="tocnumber">6.1</span> <span class="toctext">Central Atlantic</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-15"><a href="#Northern_Atlantic"><span class="tocnumber">6.2</span> <span class="toctext"><span>Northern Atlantic</span></span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-16"><a href="#South_Atlantic"><span class="tocnumber">6.3</span> <span class="toctext">South Atlantic</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-17"><a href="#Closure_of_the_Atlantic"><span class="tocnumber">6.4</span> <span class="toctext">Closure of the Atlantic</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-18"><a href="#History"><span class="tocnumber">7</span> <span class="toctext">History</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-19"><a href="#Human_origin"><span class="tocnumber">7.1</span> <span class="toctext">Human origin</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-20"><a href="#Old_World"><span class="tocnumber">7.2</span> <span class="toctext">Old World</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-21"><a href="#New_World"><span class="tocnumber">7.3</span> <span class="toctext">New World</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-22"><a href="#Atlantic_World"><span class="tocnumber">7.4</span> <span class="toctext">Atlantic World</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-23"><a href="#Economy"><span class="tocnumber">8</span> <span class="toctext">Economy</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-24"><a href="#Fisheries"><span class="tocnumber">8.1</span> <span class="toctext">Fisheries</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-25"><a href="#Environmental_issues"><span class="tocnumber">9</span> <span class="toctext">Environmental issues</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-26"><a href="#See_also"><span class="tocnumber">10</span> <span class="toctext">See also</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-27"><a href="#References"><span class="tocnumber">11</span> <span class="toctext">References</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-28"><a href="#Notes"><span class="tocnumber">11.1</span> <span class="toctext">Notes</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-29"><a href="#Sources"><span class="tocnumber">11.2</span> <span class="toctext">Sources</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-30"><a href="#Further_reading"><span class="tocnumber">12</span> <span class="toctext">Further reading</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-31"><a href="#External_links"><span class="tocnumber">13</span> <span class="toctext">External links</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</div>\n\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Etymology">Etymology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Atlantic_Ocean&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Etymology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:1710_De_La_Feuille_Map_of_Africa_-_Geographicus_-_Africa-lafeuille-1710.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/62/1710_De_La_Feuille_Map_of_Africa_-_Geographicus_-_Africa-lafeuille-1710.jpg/220px-1710_De_La_Feuille_Map_of_Africa_-_Geographicus_-_Africa-lafeuille-1710.jpg" width="220" height="152" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/62/1710_De_La_Feuille_Map_of_Africa_-_Geographicus_-_Africa-lafeuille-1710.jpg/330px-1710_De_La_Feuille_Map_of_Africa_-_Geographicus_-_Africa-lafeuille-1710.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/62/1710_De_La_Feuille_Map_of_Africa_-_Geographicus_-_Africa-lafeuille-1710.jpg/440px-1710_De_La_Feuille_Map_of_Africa_-_Geographicus_-_Africa-lafeuille-1710.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2528" data-file-height="1745" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:1710_De_La_Feuille_Map_of_Africa_-_Geographicus_-_Africa-lafeuille-1710.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Aethiopian_Ocean" class="mw-redirect" title="Aethiopian Ocean">Aethiopian Ocean</a> in a 1710 French map of Africa</div></div></div>\n<p>The oldest known mentions of an "Atlantic" sea come from <a href="/wiki/Stesichorus" title="Stesichorus">Stesichorus</a> around mid-sixth century BC (Sch. A. R. 1. 211):<sup id="cite_ref-MangasPl\xc3\xa1cido1998_6-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MangasPl\xc3\xa1cido1998-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> <i>Atlantikoi pel\xc3\xa1gei</i> (Greek: \xe1\xbc\x88\xcf\x84\xce\xbb\xce\xb1\xce\xbd\xcf\x84\xce\xb9\xce\xba\xe1\xbf\xb7 \xcf\x80\xce\xb5\xce\xbb\xce\xac\xce\xb3\xce\xb5\xce\xb9; English: \'the Atlantic sea\'; etym. \'Sea of <a href="/wiki/Atlantis" title="Atlantis">Atlantis</a>\') and in <i><a href="/wiki/Histories_(Herodotus)" title="Histories (Herodotus)">The Histories</a></i> of <a href="/wiki/Herodotus" title="Herodotus">Herodotus</a> around 450 BC (Hdt. 1.202.4): <i>Atlantis thalassa</i> (Greek: \xe1\xbc\x88\xcf\x84\xce\xbb\xce\xb1\xce\xbd\xcf\x84\xe1\xbd\xb6\xcf\x82 \xce\xb8\xce\xac\xce\xbb\xce\xb1\xcf\x83\xcf\x83\xce\xb1; English: \'Sea of Atlantis\' or \'the Atlantis sea\'<sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup>) where the name refers to "the sea beyond the <a href="/wiki/Pillars_of_Heracles" class="mw-redirect" title="Pillars of Heracles">pillars of Heracles</a>" which is said to be part of the sea that surrounds all land.<sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup>  Thus, on one hand, the name refers to <a href="/wiki/Atlas_(mythology)" title="Atlas (mythology)">Atlas</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Titan_(mythology)" title="Titan (mythology)">Titan</a> in <a href="/wiki/Greek_mythology" title="Greek mythology">Greek mythology</a>, who supported the heavens and who later appeared as a frontispiece in Medieval maps and also lent his name to modern <a href="/wiki/Atlas_(geography)" class="mw-redirect" title="Atlas (geography)">atlases</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Oxford-Dict_9-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Oxford-Dict-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup>  On the other hand, to early <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greece" title="Ancient Greece">Greek</a> sailors and in Ancient Greek mythological literature such as the <i><a href="/wiki/Iliad" title="Iliad">Iliad</a></i> and the <i><a href="/wiki/Odyssey" title="Odyssey">Odyssey</a></i>, this all-encompassing ocean was instead known as <a href="/wiki/Oceanus" title="Oceanus">Oceanus</a>, the gigantic river that encircled the world; in contrast to the enclosed seas well known to the Greeks: the Mediterranean and the Black Sea.<sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup>\nIn contrast, the term "Atlantic" originally referred specifically to the <a href="/wiki/Atlas_Mountains" title="Atlas Mountains">Atlas Mountains</a> in Morocco and the sea off the <a href="/wiki/Strait_of_Gibraltar" title="Strait of Gibraltar">Strait of Gibraltar</a> and the North African coast.<sup id="cite_ref-Oxford-Dict_9-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Oxford-Dict-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> The Greek word <i>thalassa</i> has been reused by scientists for the huge <a href="/wiki/Panthalassa" title="Panthalassa">Panthalassa</a> ocean that surrounded the supercontinent <a href="/wiki/Pangaea" title="Pangaea">Pangaea</a> hundreds of millions of years ago.\n</p><p>The term "<a href="/wiki/Aethiopian_Ocean" class="mw-redirect" title="Aethiopian Ocean">Aethiopian Ocean</a>", derived from <a href="/wiki/Aethiopia" title="Aethiopia">Ancient Ethiopia</a>, was applied to the Southern Atlantic as late as the mid-19th century.<sup id="cite_ref-AmCyclo-1873_11-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AmCyclo-1873-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup>\nDuring the <a href="/wiki/Age_of_Discovery" title="Age of Discovery">Age of Discovery</a>, the Atlantic was also known to English cartographers as the <b>Great Western Ocean</b>.<sup id="cite_ref-Steele1986_12-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Steele1986-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Extent_and_data">Extent and data</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Atlantic_Ocean&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Extent and data">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">For complete lists of marginal seas and borders of the Atlantic, see <a href="/wiki/List_of_seas#Atlantic_Ocean" title="List of seas">List of seas \xc2\xa7&#160;Atlantic Ocean</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Borders_of_the_oceans#Atlantic_Ocean" title="Borders of the oceans">Borders of the oceans \xc2\xa7&#160;Atlantic Ocean</a>.</div>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">For the extent of the Southern Ocean, see <a href="/wiki/Southern_Ocean#Definitions_and_use" title="Southern Ocean">Southern Ocean \xc2\xa7&#160;Definitions and use</a>.</div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Atlantic_Ocean_-_en.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/47/Atlantic_Ocean_-_en.png/220px-Atlantic_Ocean_-_en.png" width="220" height="220" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/4/47/Atlantic_Ocean_-_en.png 1.5x" data-file-width="328" data-file-height="328" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Atlantic_Ocean_-_en.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Extent of the Atlantic Ocean according to the 2002 IHO definition, excluding <a href="/wiki/Arctic" title="Arctic">Arctic</a> and <a href="/wiki/Antarctic" title="Antarctic">Antarctic</a> regions</div></div></div>\n<p>The <a href="/wiki/International_Hydrographic_Organization" title="International Hydrographic Organization">International Hydrographic Organization</a> (IHO) defined the limits of the oceans and seas in 1953,<sup id="cite_ref-IHO-1953_13-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IHO-1953-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup> but some of these definitions have been revised since then and some are not used by various authorities, institutions, and countries, see for example the <a href="/wiki/CIA_World_Factbook" class="mw-redirect" title="CIA World Factbook">CIA World Factbook</a>. Correspondingly, the extent and number of oceans and seas varies.\n</p><p>The Atlantic Ocean is bounded on the west by North and South America. It connects to the Arctic Ocean through the <a href="/wiki/Denmark_Strait" title="Denmark Strait">Denmark Strait</a>, <a href="/wiki/Greenland_Sea" title="Greenland Sea">Greenland Sea</a>, <a href="/wiki/Norwegian_Sea" title="Norwegian Sea">Norwegian Sea</a> and <a href="/wiki/Barents_Sea" title="Barents Sea">Barents Sea</a>. To the east, the boundaries of the ocean proper are Europe: the <a href="/wiki/Strait_of_Gibraltar" title="Strait of Gibraltar">Strait of Gibraltar</a> (where it connects with the <a href="/wiki/Mediterranean_Sea" title="Mediterranean Sea">Mediterranean Sea</a>\xe2\x80\x93one of its <a href="/wiki/Marginal_sea" class="mw-redirect" title="Marginal sea">marginal seas</a>\xe2\x80\x93and, in turn, the <a href="/wiki/Black_Sea" title="Black Sea">Black Sea</a>, both of which also touch upon Asia) and Africa.\n</p><p>In the southeast, the Atlantic merges into the Indian Ocean. The <a href="/wiki/20th_meridian_east" title="20th meridian east">20\xc2\xb0 East meridian</a>, running south from <a href="/wiki/Cape_Agulhas" title="Cape Agulhas">Cape Agulhas</a> to <a href="/wiki/Antarctica" title="Antarctica">Antarctica</a> defines its border. In the 1953 definition it extends south to Antarctica, while in later maps it is bounded at the <a href="/wiki/60th_parallel_south" title="60th parallel south">60\xc2\xb0 parallel</a> by the Southern Ocean.<sup id="cite_ref-IHO-1953_13-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IHO-1953-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The Atlantic has irregular coasts indented by numerous bays, gulfs and seas. These include the <a href="/wiki/Baltic_Sea" title="Baltic Sea">Baltic Sea</a>, <a href="/wiki/Black_Sea" title="Black Sea">Black Sea</a>, <a href="/wiki/Caribbean_Sea" title="Caribbean Sea">Caribbean Sea</a>, <a href="/wiki/Davis_Strait" title="Davis Strait">Davis Strait</a>, <a href="/wiki/Denmark_Strait" title="Denmark Strait">Denmark Strait</a>, part of the <a href="/wiki/Drake_Passage" title="Drake Passage">Drake Passage</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gulf_of_Mexico" title="Gulf of Mexico">Gulf of Mexico</a>, <a href="/wiki/Labrador_Sea" title="Labrador Sea">Labrador Sea</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mediterranean_Sea" title="Mediterranean Sea">Mediterranean Sea</a>, <a href="/wiki/North_Sea" title="North Sea">North Sea</a>, <a href="/wiki/Norwegian_Sea" title="Norwegian Sea">Norwegian Sea</a>, almost all of the <a href="/wiki/Scotia_Sea" title="Scotia Sea">Scotia Sea</a>, and other tributary water bodies.<sup id="cite_ref-CIA-World_1-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CIA-World-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup>  Including these marginal seas the coast line of the Atlantic measures 111,866&#160;km (69,510&#160;mi) compared to 135,663&#160;km (84,297&#160;mi) for the Pacific.<sup id="cite_ref-CIA-World_1-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CIA-World-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Including its marginal seas, the Atlantic covers an area of 106,460,000&#160;km<sup>2</sup> (41,100,000&#160;sq&#160;mi) or 23.5% of the global ocean and has a volume of 310,410,900&#160;km<sup>3</sup> (74,471,500&#160;cu&#160;mi) or 23.3% of the total volume of the earth\'s oceans. Excluding its marginal seas, the Atlantic covers 81,760,000&#160;km<sup>2</sup> (31,570,000&#160;sq&#160;mi) and has a volume of 305,811,900&#160;km<sup>3</sup> (73,368,200&#160;cu&#160;mi). The North Atlantic covers 41,490,000&#160;km<sup>2</sup> (16,020,000&#160;sq&#160;mi) (11.5%) and the South Atlantic 40,270,000&#160;km<sup>2</sup> (15,550,000&#160;sq&#160;mi) (11.1%).<sup id="cite_ref-ETOPO1_4-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ETOPO1-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> The average depth is 3,646&#160;m (11,962&#160;ft) and the maximum depth, the <a href="/wiki/Milwaukee_Deep" title="Milwaukee Deep">Milwaukee Deep</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Puerto_Rico_Trench" title="Puerto Rico Trench">Puerto Rico Trench</a>, is 8,486&#160;m (27,841&#160;ft).<sup id="cite_ref-ETOPO1_4-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ETOPO1-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Bathymetry">Bathymetry</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Atlantic_Ocean&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Bathymetry">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Atlantic_bathymetry.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ce/Atlantic_bathymetry.jpg/220px-Atlantic_bathymetry.jpg" width="220" height="241" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ce/Atlantic_bathymetry.jpg/330px-Atlantic_bathymetry.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ce/Atlantic_bathymetry.jpg/440px-Atlantic_bathymetry.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1825" data-file-height="2000" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Atlantic_bathymetry.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/False_color" title="False color">False color</a> map of ocean depth in the Atlantic basin</div></div></div>\n<p>The <a href="/wiki/Bathymetry" title="Bathymetry">bathymetry</a> of the Atlantic is dominated by a <a href="/wiki/Mid-ocean_ridge" title="Mid-ocean ridge">submarine mountain range</a> called the <a href="/wiki/Mid-Atlantic_Ridge" title="Mid-Atlantic Ridge">Mid-Atlantic Ridge</a> (MAR). It runs from 87\xc2\xb0N or 300&#160;km (190&#160;mi) south of the <a href="/wiki/North_Pole" title="North Pole">North Pole</a> to the subantarctic <a href="/wiki/Bouvet_Island" title="Bouvet Island">Bouvet Island</a> at <a href="/wiki/42nd_parallel_south" title="42nd parallel south">42\xc2\xb0S</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-WHC-MAR_16-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WHC-MAR-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Mid-Atlantic_Ridge">Mid-Atlantic Ridge</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Atlantic_Ocean&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Mid-Atlantic Ridge">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Mid-Atlantic_Ridge" title="Mid-Atlantic Ridge">Mid-Atlantic Ridge</a></div>\n<p>The MAR divides the Atlantic longitudinally into two halves, in each of which a series of basins are delimited by secondary, transverse ridges.  The MAR reaches above 2,000&#160;m (6,600&#160;ft) along most of its length, but is interrupted by larger transform faults at two places: the <a href="/wiki/Romanche_Trench" title="Romanche Trench">Romanche Trench</a> near the Equator and the <a href="/wiki/Gibbs_Fracture_Zone" class="mw-redirect" title="Gibbs Fracture Zone">Gibbs Fracture Zone</a> at <a href="/wiki/53rd_parallel_north" title="53rd parallel north">53\xc2\xb0N</a>. The MAR is a barrier for bottom water, but at these two transform faults deep water currents can pass from one side to the other.<sup id="cite_ref-LevGood-2003_17-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-LevGood-2003-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The MAR rises 2\xe2\x80\x933&#160;km (1.2\xe2\x80\x931.9&#160;mi) above the surrounding ocean floor and its <a href="/wiki/Rift_valley" title="Rift valley">rift valley</a> is the <a href="/wiki/Divergent_boundary" title="Divergent boundary">divergent boundary</a> between the <a href="/wiki/North_American_Plate" title="North American Plate">North American</a> and <a href="/wiki/Eurasian_Plate" title="Eurasian Plate">Eurasian</a> plates in the North Atlantic and the <a href="/wiki/South_American_Plate" title="South American Plate">South American</a> and <a href="/wiki/African_Plate" title="African Plate">African</a> plates in the South Atlantic. The MAR produces <a href="/wiki/Basaltic_lava" class="mw-redirect" title="Basaltic lava">basaltic volcanoes</a> in <a href="/wiki/Eyjafjallaj%C3%B6kull" title="Eyjafjallaj\xc3\xb6kull">Eyjafjallaj\xc3\xb6kull</a>, Iceland, and <a href="/wiki/Pillow_lava" title="Pillow lava">pillow lava</a> on the ocean floor.<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup> The depth of water at the apex of the ridge is less than 2,700&#160;m (1,500 <a href="/wiki/Fathom" title="Fathom">fathoms</a>; 8,900&#160;<a href="/wiki/Foot_(unit)" title="Foot (unit)">ft</a>) in most places, while the bottom of the ridge is three times as deep.<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The MAR is intersected by two perpendicular ridges: the <a href="/wiki/Azores%E2%80%93Gibraltar_Transform_Fault" title="Azores\xe2\x80\x93Gibraltar Transform Fault">Azores\xe2\x80\x93Gibraltar Transform Fault</a>, the boundary between the <a href="/wiki/Nubian_Plate" class="mw-redirect" title="Nubian Plate">Nubian</a> and <a href="/wiki/Eurasian_Plate" title="Eurasian Plate">Eurasian plates</a>, intersects the MAR at the <a href="/wiki/Azores_Triple_Junction" title="Azores Triple Junction">Azores Triple Junction</a>, on either side of the Azores microplate, near the <a href="/wiki/40th_parallel_north" title="40th parallel north">40\xc2\xb0N</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup> A much vaguer, nameless boundary, between the <a href="/wiki/North_American_Plate" title="North American Plate">North American</a> and <a href="/wiki/South_American_Plate" title="South American Plate">South American</a> plates, intersects the MAR near or just north of the <a href="/wiki/Fifteen-Twenty_Fracture_Zone" title="Fifteen-Twenty Fracture Zone">Fifteen-Twenty Fracture Zone</a>, approximately at <a href="/wiki/16th_parallel_north" title="16th parallel north">16\xc2\xb0N</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In the 1870s, the <a href="/wiki/Challenger_expedition" title="Challenger expedition">Challenger expedition</a> discovered parts of what is now known as the Mid-Atlantic Ridge, or:\n</p>\n<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r856303468">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>An elevated ridge rising to an average height of about 1,900 fathoms&#160;[3,500&#160;m; 11,400&#160;ft] below the surface traverses the basins of the North and South Atlantic in a meridianal direction from Cape Farewell, probably its far south at least as Gough Island, following roughly the outlines of the coasts of the Old and the New Worlds.<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup>\n</p></blockquote><p>  The remainder of the ridge was discovered in the 1920s by the <a href="/wiki/German_Meteor_expedition" title="German Meteor expedition">German Meteor expedition</a> using echo-sounding equipment.<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup> The exploration of the MAR in the 1950s led to the general acceptance of <a href="/wiki/Seafloor_spreading" title="Seafloor spreading">seafloor spreading</a> and <a href="/wiki/Plate_tectonics" title="Plate tectonics">plate tectonics</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-WHC-MAR_16-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WHC-MAR-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Most of the MAR runs under water but where it reaches the surfaces it has produced volcanic islands. While nine of these have collectively been nominated a <a href="/wiki/World_Heritage_Site" class="mw-redirect" title="World Heritage Site">World Heritage Site</a> for their geological value, four of them are considered of "Outstanding Universal Value" based on their cultural and natural criteria: <a href="/wiki/%C3%9Eingvellir_National_Park" class="mw-redirect" title="\xc3\x9eingvellir National Park">\xc3\x9eingvellir</a>, Iceland; <a href="/wiki/Landscape_of_the_Pico_Island_Vineyard_Culture" title="Landscape of the Pico Island Vineyard Culture">Landscape of the Pico Island Vineyard Culture</a>, Portugal; <a href="/wiki/Gough_and_Inaccessible_Islands" title="Gough and Inaccessible Islands">Gough and Inaccessible Islands</a>, United Kingdom; and Brazilian Atlantic Islands: <a href="/wiki/Fernando_de_Noronha" title="Fernando de Noronha">Fernando de Noronha</a> and <a href="/wiki/Atol_das_Rocas" class="mw-redirect" title="Atol das Rocas">Atol das Rocas</a> Reserves, Brazil.<sup id="cite_ref-WHC-MAR_16-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WHC-MAR-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ocean_floor">Ocean floor</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Atlantic_Ocean&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Ocean floor">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">For a list of fracture zones in the Atlantic, see <a href="/wiki/List_of_fracture_zones#Atlantic_Ocean" title="List of fracture zones">List of fracture zones \xc2\xa7&#160;Atlantic Ocean</a>.</div>\n<p>Continental shelves in the Atlantic are wide off Newfoundland, southern-most South America, and north-eastern Europe.\nIn the western Atlantic <a href="/wiki/Carbonate_platform" title="Carbonate platform">carbonate platforms</a> dominate large areas, for example the <a href="/wiki/Blake_Plateau" title="Blake Plateau">Blake Plateau</a> and <a href="/w/index.php?title=Bermuda_Rise&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Bermuda Rise (page does not exist)">Bermuda Rise</a>.\nThe Atlantic is surrounded by <a href="/wiki/Passive_margin" title="Passive margin">passive margins</a> except at a few locations where <a href="/wiki/Active_margin" class="mw-redirect" title="Active margin">active margins</a> form deep <a href="/wiki/Oceanic_trench" title="Oceanic trench">trenches</a>: the <a href="/wiki/Puerto_Rico_Trench" title="Puerto Rico Trench">Puerto Rico Trench</a> (8,414&#160;m or 27,605&#160;ft maximum depth) in the western Pacific and <a href="/wiki/South_Sandwich_Trench" title="South Sandwich Trench">South Sandwich Trench</a> (8,264&#160;m or 27,113&#160;ft) in the South Atlantic.  There are numerous submarine canyons off north-eastern North America, western Europe, and north-western Africa. Some of these canyons extend along the continental rises and farther into the abyssal plains as deep-sea channels.<sup id="cite_ref-LevGood-2003_17-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-LevGood-2003-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 1922 a historic moment in cartography and oceanography occurred. The USS Stewart used a Navy Sonic Depth Finder to draw a continuous map across the bed of the Atlantic. This involved little guesswork because the idea of sonar is straight forward with pulses being sent from the vessel, which bounce off the ocean floor, then return to the vessel.<sup id="cite_ref-the_Atlantic_24-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-the_Atlantic-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup>  The deep ocean floor is thought to be fairly flat with occasional deeps, <a href="/wiki/Abyssal_plain" title="Abyssal plain">abyssal plains</a>, <a href="/wiki/Oceanic_trench" title="Oceanic trench">trenches</a>, <a href="/wiki/Seamount" title="Seamount">seamounts</a>, <a href="/wiki/Oceanic_basin" title="Oceanic basin">basins</a>, <a href="/wiki/Oceanic_plateau" title="Oceanic plateau">plateaus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Submarine_canyon" title="Submarine canyon">canyons</a>, and some <a href="/wiki/Guyot" title="Guyot">guyots</a>. Various shelves along the margins of the continents constitute about 11% of the bottom topography with few deep channels cut across the continental rise.\n</p><p>The mean depth between <a href="/wiki/60th_parallel_north" title="60th parallel north">60\xc2\xb0N</a> and <a href="/wiki/60th_parallel_south" title="60th parallel south">60\xc2\xb0S</a> is 3,730&#160;m (12,240&#160;ft), or close to the average for the global ocean, with a modal depth between 4,000 and 5,000&#160;m (13,000 and 16,000&#160;ft).<sup id="cite_ref-LevGood-2003_17-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-LevGood-2003-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In the South Atlantic the <a href="/wiki/Walvis_Ridge" title="Walvis Ridge">Walvis Ridge</a> and <a href="/wiki/Rio_Grande_Rise" title="Rio Grande Rise">Rio Grande Rise</a> form barriers to ocean currents.\nThe <a href="/wiki/Laurentian_Abyss" class="mw-redirect" title="Laurentian Abyss">Laurentian Abyss</a> is found off the eastern coast of Canada.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Water_characteristics">Water characteristics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Atlantic_Ocean&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Water characteristics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:402px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Gulf_Stream_Sea_Surface_Currents_and_Temperatures_NASA_SVS.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Visualisation of the Gulf Stream stretching from the Gulf of Mexico to Western Europe" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/99/Gulf_Stream_Sea_Surface_Currents_and_Temperatures_NASA_SVS.jpg/400px-Gulf_Stream_Sea_Surface_Currents_and_Temperatures_NASA_SVS.jpg" width="400" height="135" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/99/Gulf_Stream_Sea_Surface_Currents_and_Temperatures_NASA_SVS.jpg/600px-Gulf_Stream_Sea_Surface_Currents_and_Temperatures_NASA_SVS.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/99/Gulf_Stream_Sea_Surface_Currents_and_Temperatures_NASA_SVS.jpg/800px-Gulf_Stream_Sea_Surface_Currents_and_Temperatures_NASA_SVS.jpg 2x" data-file-width="6840" data-file-height="2304" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Gulf_Stream_Sea_Surface_Currents_and_Temperatures_NASA_SVS.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>As the Gulf Stream meanders across the North Atlantic from the North American east coast to Western Europe its temperature drops by 20&#160;\xc2\xb0C (36&#160;\xc2\xb0F).</div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Circulacion_termohalina.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Map displaying a looping line with arrows indicating that water flows eastward in the far Southern ocean, angling north east of Australia, turning sough after passing Alaska, then crossing the mid-Pacific to flow north of Australia, continuing west below Africa, then turning northwest until reaching eastern Canada, then angling east to southern Europe, then finally turning south just below Greenland and flowing down the Americas&#39; eastern coast, and resuming its flow eastward to complete the circle" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Circulacion_termohalina.jpg/220px-Circulacion_termohalina.jpg" width="220" height="110" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Circulacion_termohalina.jpg/330px-Circulacion_termohalina.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Circulacion_termohalina.jpg/440px-Circulacion_termohalina.jpg 2x" data-file-width="800" data-file-height="400" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Circulacion_termohalina.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Path of the <a href="/wiki/Thermohaline_circulation" title="Thermohaline circulation">thermohaline circulation</a>. Purple paths represent deep-water currents, while blue paths represent surface currents.</div></div></div>\n<p>Surface water temperatures, which vary with latitude, current systems, and season and reflect the latitudinal distribution of solar energy, range from below \xe2\x88\x922&#160;\xc2\xb0C (28&#160;\xc2\xb0F) to over 30&#160;\xc2\xb0C (86&#160;\xc2\xb0F). Maximum temperatures occur north of the equator, and minimum values are found in the polar regions. In the middle latitudes, the area of maximum temperature variations, values may vary by 7\xe2\x80\x938&#160;\xc2\xb0C (13\xe2\x80\x9314&#160;\xc2\xb0F).<sup id="cite_ref-USN-2001_5-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-USN-2001-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>From October to June the surface is usually covered with sea ice in the <a href="/wiki/Labrador_Sea" title="Labrador Sea">Labrador Sea</a>, <a href="/wiki/Denmark_Strait" title="Denmark Strait">Denmark Strait</a>, and Baltic Sea.<sup id="cite_ref-USN-2001_5-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-USN-2001-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Coriolis_effect" class="mw-redirect" title="Coriolis effect">Coriolis effect</a> circulates North Atlantic water in a clockwise direction, whereas South Atlantic water circulates counter-clockwise. The south <a href="/wiki/Tide" title="Tide">tides</a> in the Atlantic Ocean are semi-diurnal; that is, two high tides occur during each 24 lunar hours. In latitudes above <a href="/wiki/40th_parallel_north" title="40th parallel north">40\xc2\xb0 North</a> some east-west oscillation, known as the <a href="/wiki/North_Atlantic_oscillation" title="North Atlantic oscillation">North Atlantic oscillation</a>, occurs.<sup id="cite_ref-USN-2001_5-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-USN-2001-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Salinity">Salinity</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Atlantic_Ocean&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Salinity">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>On average, the Atlantic is the saltiest major ocean; surface water <a href="/wiki/Salinity" title="Salinity">salinity</a> in the open ocean ranges from 33 to 37 parts per thousand (3.3 \xe2\x80\x93 3.7%) by mass and varies with latitude and season. Evaporation, precipitation, river inflow and <a href="/wiki/Sea_ice" title="Sea ice">sea ice</a> melting influence surface salinity values. Although the lowest salinity values are just north of the equator (because of heavy tropical rainfall), in general the lowest values are in the high latitudes and along coasts where large rivers enter. Maximum salinity values occur at about <a href="/wiki/25th_parallel_north" title="25th parallel north">25\xc2\xb0 north</a> and <a href="/wiki/25th_parallel_south" title="25th parallel south">south</a>, in <a href="/wiki/Subtropical" class="mw-redirect" title="Subtropical">subtropical</a> regions with low rainfall and high evaporation.<sup id="cite_ref-USN-2001_5-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-USN-2001-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The high surface salinity in the Atlantic, on which the Atlantic <a href="/wiki/Thermohaline_circulation" title="Thermohaline circulation">thermohaline circulation</a> is dependent, is maintained by two processes: the <a href="/wiki/Agulhas_Current#Agulhas_leakage_and_rings" title="Agulhas Current">Agulhas Leakage/Rings</a>, which brings salty Indian Ocean waters into the South Atlantic, and the "Atmospheric Bridge", which evaporates subtropical Atlantic waters and exports it to the Pacific.<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div style="clear:both;"></div>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Water_masses">Water masses</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Atlantic_Ocean&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Water masses">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<table class="wikitable floatright" style="font-size: 0.9em; text-align: center;">\n<caption>Temperature-salinity characteristics for Atlantic water masses<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup>\n</caption>\n<tbody><tr>\n<th>Water mass</th>\n<th>Temperature</th>\n<th>Salinity\n</th></tr>\n<tr>\n<th colspan="3">Upper waters (0\xe2\x80\x93500&#160;m or 0\xe2\x80\x931,600&#160;ft)\n</th></tr>\n<tr>\n<td align="left">Atlantic Subarctic<br />Upper Water (ASUW)</td>\n<td>0.0\xe2\x80\x934.0&#160;\xc2\xb0C</td>\n<td>34.0\xe2\x80\x9335.0\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td align="left">Western North Atlantic<br />Central Water (WNACW)</td>\n<td>7.0\xe2\x80\x9320&#160;\xc2\xb0C</td>\n<td>35.0\xe2\x80\x9336.7\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td align="left">Eastern North Atlantic<br />Central Water (ENACW)</td>\n<td>8.0\xe2\x80\x9318.0&#160;\xc2\xb0C</td>\n<td>35.2\xe2\x80\x9336.7\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td align="left">South Atlantic<br />Central Water (SACW)</td>\n<td>5.0\xe2\x80\x9318.0&#160;\xc2\xb0C</td>\n<td>34.3\xe2\x80\x9335.8\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<th colspan="3">Intermediate waters (500\xe2\x80\x931,500&#160;m or 1,600\xe2\x80\x934,900&#160;ft)\n</th></tr>\n<tr>\n<td align="left">Western Atlantic Subarctic<br />Intermediate Water (WASIW)</td>\n<td>3.0\xe2\x80\x939.0&#160;\xc2\xb0C</td>\n<td>34.0\xe2\x80\x9335.1\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td align="left">Eastern Atlantic Subarctic<br />Intermediate Water (EASIW)</td>\n<td>3.0\xe2\x80\x939.0&#160;\xc2\xb0C</td>\n<td>34.4\xe2\x80\x9335.3\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td align="left">Mediterranean Water (MW)</td>\n<td>2.6\xe2\x80\x9311.0&#160;\xc2\xb0C</td>\n<td>35.0\xe2\x80\x9336.2\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td align="left">Arctic Intermediate Water (AIW)</td>\n<td>\xe2\x88\x921.5\xe2\x80\x933.0&#160;\xc2\xb0C</td>\n<td>34.7\xe2\x80\x9334.9\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<th colspan="3">Deep and abyssal waters (1,500&#160;m\xe2\x80\x93bottom or 4,900&#160;ft\xe2\x80\x93bottom)\n</th></tr>\n<tr>\n<td align="left">North Atlantic<br />Deep Water (NADW)</td>\n<td>1.5\xe2\x80\x934.0&#160;\xc2\xb0C</td>\n<td>34.8\xe2\x80\x9335.0\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td align="left">Antarctic Bottom Water (AABW)</td>\n<td>\xe2\x88\x920.9\xe2\x80\x931.7&#160;\xc2\xb0C</td>\n<td>34.64\xe2\x80\x9334.72\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td align="left">Arctic Bottom Water (ABW)</td>\n<td>\xe2\x88\x921.8 to \xe2\x88\x920.5&#160;\xc2\xb0C</td>\n<td>34.85\xe2\x80\x9334.94\n</td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p>The Atlantic Ocean consists of four major, upper <a href="/wiki/Water_mass" title="Water mass">water masses</a> with distinct temperature and salinity. The Atlantic Subarctic Upper Water in the northern-most North Atlantic is the source for Subarctic Intermediate Water and North Atlantic Intermediate Water. North Atlantic Central Water can be divided into the Eastern and Western North Atlantic central Water since the western part is strongly affected by the Gulf Stream and therefore the upper layer is closer to underlying fresher subpolar intermediate water. The eastern water is saltier because of its proximity to Mediterranean Water. North Atlantic Central Water flows into South Atlantic Central Water at <a href="/wiki/15th_parallel_north" title="15th parallel north">15\xc2\xb0N</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Emery-Atlantic_27-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Emery-Atlantic-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>There are five intermediate waters: four low-salinity waters formed at subpolar latitudes and one high-salinity formed through evaporation. Arctic Intermediate Water, flows from north to become the source for North Atlantic Deep Water south of the Greenland-Scotland sill. These two intermediate waters have different salinity in the western and eastern basins. The wide range of salinities in the North Atlantic is caused by the asymmetry of the northern subtropical gyre and the large number of contributions from a wide range of sources: Labrador Sea, Norwegian-Greenland Sea, Mediterranean, and South Atlantic Intermediate Water.<sup id="cite_ref-Emery-Atlantic_27-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Emery-Atlantic-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/North_Atlantic_Deep_Water" title="North Atlantic Deep Water">North Atlantic Deep Water</a> (NADW) is a complex of four water masses, two that form by deep convection in the open ocean&#160;\xe2\x80\x94 Classical and Upper Labrador Sea Water&#160;\xe2\x80\x94 and two that form from the inflow of dense water across the Greenland-Iceland-Scotland sill&#160;\xe2\x80\x94 Denmark Strait and Iceland-Scotland Overflow Water. Along its path across Earth the composition of the NADW is affected by other water masses, especially <a href="/wiki/Antarctic_Bottom_Water" class="mw-redirect" title="Antarctic Bottom Water">Antarctic Bottom Water</a> and Mediterranean Overflow Water.<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup>\nThe NADW is fed by a flow of warm shallow water into the northern North Atlantic which is responsible for the anomalous warm climate in Europe. Changes in the formation of NADW have been linked to global climate changes in the past. Since man-made substances were introduced into the environment, the path of the NADW can be traced throughout its course by measuring tritium and radiocarbon from <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_weapon_test" class="mw-redirect" title="Nuclear weapon test">nuclear weapon tests</a> in the 1960s and <a href="/wiki/Chlorofluorocarbon" title="Chlorofluorocarbon">CFCs</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div style="clear:both;"></div>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Gyres">Gyres</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Atlantic_Ocean&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Gyres">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Oceanic_gyres.png" class="image"><img alt="Map showing 5 circles. The first is between western Australia and eastern Africa. The second is between eastern Australia and western South America. The third is between Japan and western North America. Of the two in the Atlantic, one is in hemisphere." src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Oceanic_gyres.png/220px-Oceanic_gyres.png" width="220" height="144" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Oceanic_gyres.png/330px-Oceanic_gyres.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Oceanic_gyres.png/440px-Oceanic_gyres.png 2x" data-file-width="450" data-file-height="294" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Oceanic_gyres.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Map of the five major ocean <a href="/wiki/Ocean_gyre" title="Ocean gyre">gyres</a></div></div></div>\n<p>The clockwise warm-water <a href="/wiki/North_Atlantic_Gyre" title="North Atlantic Gyre">North Atlantic Gyre</a> occupies the northern Atlantic, and the counter-clockwise warm-water <a href="/wiki/South_Atlantic_Gyre" title="South Atlantic Gyre">South Atlantic Gyre</a> appears in the southern Atlantic.<sup id="cite_ref-USN-2001_5-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-USN-2001-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In the North Atlantic, surface circulation is dominated by three inter-connected currents: the <a href="/wiki/Gulf_Stream" title="Gulf Stream">Gulf Stream</a> which flows north-east from the North American coast at <a href="/wiki/Cape_Hatteras" title="Cape Hatteras">Cape Hatteras</a>; the <a href="/wiki/North_Atlantic_Current" title="North Atlantic Current">North Atlantic Current</a>, a branch of the Gulf Stream which flows northward from the <a href="/wiki/Grand_Banks_of_Newfoundland" title="Grand Banks of Newfoundland">Grand Banks</a>; and the <a href="/w/index.php?title=Subpolar_Front&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Subpolar Front (page does not exist)">Subpolar Front</a>, an extension of the North Atlantic Current, a wide, vaguely defined region separating the subtropical gyre from the subpolar gyre.  This system of currents transport warm water into the North Atlantic, without which temperatures in the North Atlantic and Europe would plunge dramatically.<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:North_Atlantic_Circulation.gif" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/c/c5/North_Atlantic_Circulation.gif/220px-North_Atlantic_Circulation.gif" width="220" height="187" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/c/c5/North_Atlantic_Circulation.gif/330px-North_Atlantic_Circulation.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/c/c5/North_Atlantic_Circulation.gif/440px-North_Atlantic_Circulation.gif 2x" data-file-width="548" data-file-height="465" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:North_Atlantic_Circulation.gif" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>In the subpolar gyre of the North Atlantic warm subtropical waters are transformed into colder subpolar and polar waters.  In the Labrador Sea this water flows back to the subtropical gyre.</div></div></div>\n<p>North of the North Atlantic Gyre, the cyclonic <a href="/w/index.php?title=North_Atlantic_Subpolar_Gyre&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="North Atlantic Subpolar Gyre (page does not exist)">North Atlantic Subpolar Gyre</a> plays a key role in climate variability.  It is governed by ocean currents from marginal seas and regional topography, rather than being steered by wind, both in the deep ocean and at sea level.<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup>\nThe subpolar gyre forms an important part of the global <a href="/wiki/Thermohaline_circulation" title="Thermohaline circulation">thermohaline circulation</a>.  Its eastern portion includes <a href="/wiki/Eddy_(fluid_dynamics)" title="Eddy (fluid dynamics)">eddying</a> branches of the <a href="/wiki/North_Atlantic_Current" title="North Atlantic Current">North Atlantic Current</a> which transport warm, saline waters from the subtropics to the north-eastern Atlantic.  There this water is cooled during winter and forms return currents that merge along the eastern continental slope of Greenland where they form an intense (40\xe2\x80\x9350&#160;<a href="/wiki/Sverdrup" title="Sverdrup">Sv</a>) current which flows around the continental margins of the <a href="/wiki/Labrador_Sea" title="Labrador Sea">Labrador Sea</a>.  A third of this water become parts of the deep portion of the <a href="/wiki/North_Atlantic_Deep_Water" title="North Atlantic Deep Water">North Atlantic Deep Water</a> (NADW).  The NADW, in its turn, feed the <a href="/wiki/Meridional_overturning_circulation" class="mw-redirect" title="Meridional overturning circulation">meridional overturning circulation</a> (MOC), the northward heat transport of which is threatened by anthropogenic climate change.  Large variations in the subpolar gyre on a decade-century scale, associated with the <a href="/wiki/North_Atlantic_oscillation" title="North Atlantic oscillation">North Atlantic oscillation</a>, are especially pronounced in <a href="/wiki/Labrador_Sea_Water" title="Labrador Sea Water">Labrador Sea Water</a>, the upper layers of the MOC.<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The South Atlantic is dominated by the anti-cyclonic southern subtropical gyre.  The <a href="/w/index.php?title=South_Atlantic_Central_Water&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="South Atlantic Central Water (page does not exist)">South Atlantic Central Water</a> originates in this gyre, while <a href="/wiki/Antarctic_Intermediate_Water" title="Antarctic Intermediate Water">Antarctic Intermediate Water</a> originates in the upper layers of the circumpolar region, near the <a href="/wiki/Drake_Passage" title="Drake Passage">Drake Passage</a> and Falkland Islands.  Both these currents receive some contribution from the Indian Ocean.  On the African east coast the small cyclonic <a href="/w/index.php?title=Angola_Gyre&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Angola Gyre (page does not exist)">Angola Gyre</a> lies embedded in the large subtropical gyre.<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup>\nThe southern subtropical gyre is partly masked by a wind-induced <a href="/wiki/Ekman_layer" title="Ekman layer">Ekman layer</a>.  The residence time of the gyre is 4.4\xe2\x80\x938.5&#160;years. <a href="/wiki/North_Atlantic_Deep_Water" title="North Atlantic Deep Water">North Atlantic Deep Water</a> flows southerward below the <a href="/wiki/Thermocline" title="Thermocline">thermocline</a> of the subtropical gyre.<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Sargasso_Sea">Sargasso Sea</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Atlantic_Ocean&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Sargasso Sea">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Sargasso_Sea" title="Sargasso Sea">Sargasso Sea</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Sargasso.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/Sargasso.png/220px-Sargasso.png" width="220" height="142" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/Sargasso.png/330px-Sargasso.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/Sargasso.png/440px-Sargasso.png 2x" data-file-width="514" data-file-height="331" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Sargasso.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Approximate extent of the Sargasso Sea</div></div></div>\n<p>The Sargasso Sea in the western North Atlantic can be defined as the area where two species of <i><a href="/wiki/Sargassum" title="Sargassum">Sargassum</a></i> (<i>S. fluitans</i> and <i>natans</i>) float, an area 4,000&#160;km (2,500&#160;mi) wide and encircled by the <a href="/wiki/Gulf_Stream" title="Gulf Stream">Gulf Stream</a>, <a href="/wiki/North_Atlantic_Drift" class="mw-redirect" title="North Atlantic Drift">North Atlantic Drift</a>, and <a href="/wiki/North_Equatorial_Current" title="North Equatorial Current">North Equatorial Current</a>.  This population of seaweed probably originated from Tertiary ancestors on the European shores of the former <a href="/wiki/Tethys_Ocean" title="Tethys Ocean">Tethys Ocean</a> and has, if so, maintained itself by <a href="/wiki/Vegetative_reproduction" title="Vegetative reproduction">vegetative growth</a>, floating in the ocean for millions of years.<sup id="cite_ref-L\xc3\xbcn-p223_35-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-L\xc3\xbcn-p223-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:HanaOZ.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/HanaOZ.jpg/220px-HanaOZ.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/HanaOZ.jpg/330px-HanaOZ.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/HanaOZ.jpg/440px-HanaOZ.jpg 2x" data-file-width="640" data-file-height="480" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:HanaOZ.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Sargassum fish (<i>Histrio histrio</i>)</div></div></div>\n<p>Other species endemic to the Sargasso Sea include the <a href="/wiki/Sargassum_fish" title="Sargassum fish">sargassum fish</a>, a predator with algae-like appendages who hovers motionless among the <i>Sargassum</i>.  Fossils of similar fishes have been found in fossil bays of the former Tethys Ocean, in what is now the <a href="/wiki/Carpathian_Mountains" title="Carpathian Mountains">Carpathian</a> region, that were similar to the Sargasso Sea.  It is possible that the population in the Sargasso Sea migrated to the Atlantic as the Tethys closed at the end of the Miocene around 17&#160;Ma.<sup id="cite_ref-L\xc3\xbcn-p223_35-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-L\xc3\xbcn-p223-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup>\nThe origin of the Sargasso fauna and flora remained enigmatic for centuries.  The fossils found in the Carpathians in the mid-20th century, often called the "quasi-Sargasso assemblage", finally showed that this assemblage originated in the <a href="/wiki/Carpathian_Basin" class="mw-redirect" title="Carpathian Basin">Carpathian Basin</a> from were it migrated over <a href="/wiki/Sicily" title="Sicily">Sicily</a> to the Central Atlantic where it evolved into modern species of the Sargasso Sea.<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The location of the spawning ground for European eels <a href="/wiki/Eel_life_history#Search_for_the_spawning_grounds" title="Eel life history">remained unknown for decades</a>.  In the early 19th century it was discovered that the southern Sargasso Sea is the spawning ground for both the <a href="/wiki/European_eel" title="European eel">European</a> and <a href="/wiki/American_eel" title="American eel">American eel</a> and that the former migrate more than 5,000&#160;km (3,100&#160;mi) and the latter 2,000&#160;km (1,200&#160;mi).  Ocean currents such as the Gulf Stream transport eel larvae from the Sargasso Sea to foraging areas in North America, Europe, and Northern Africa.<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup> Recent but disputed research suggests that eels possibly use <a href="/wiki/Earth%27s_magnetic_field" title="Earth&#39;s magnetic field">Earth\'s magnetic field</a> to navigate through the ocean both as larvae and as adults.<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Climate">Climate</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Atlantic_Ocean&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Climate">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Atlantic_hurricane_graphic.png" class="image"><img alt="Map of Caribbean showing seven approximately parallel westward-pointing arrows that extend from east of the Virgin Islands to Cuba. The southern arrows bend northward just east of the Dominican Republic before straightening out again." src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/Atlantic_hurricane_graphic.png/220px-Atlantic_hurricane_graphic.png" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/Atlantic_hurricane_graphic.png/330px-Atlantic_hurricane_graphic.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/0/0e/Atlantic_hurricane_graphic.png 2x" data-file-width="350" data-file-height="263" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Atlantic_hurricane_graphic.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Waves in the <a href="/wiki/Trade_winds" title="Trade winds">trade winds</a> in the Atlantic Ocean\xe2\x80\x94areas of converging winds that move along the same track as the prevailing wind\xe2\x80\x94create instabilities in the atmosphere that may lead to the formation of hurricanes.</div></div></div>\n<p>Climate is influenced by the temperatures of the surface waters and water currents as well as winds. Because of the ocean\'s great capacity to store and release heat, maritime climates are more moderate and have less extreme seasonal variations than inland climates. <a href="/wiki/Precipitation_(meteorology)" class="mw-redirect" title="Precipitation (meteorology)">Precipitation</a> can be approximated from coastal weather data and air temperature from water temperatures.<sup id="cite_ref-USN-2001_5-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-USN-2001-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The oceans are the major source of the atmospheric moisture that is obtained through evaporation. Climatic zones vary with latitude; the warmest zones stretch across the Atlantic north of the equator. The coldest zones are in high latitudes, with the coldest regions corresponding to the areas covered by sea ice. Ocean currents influence climate by transporting warm and cold waters to other regions. The winds that are cooled or warmed when blowing over these currents influence adjacent land areas.<sup id="cite_ref-USN-2001_5-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-USN-2001-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Gulf_Stream" title="Gulf Stream">Gulf Stream</a> and its northern extension towards Europe, the <a href="/wiki/North_Atlantic_Current" title="North Atlantic Current">North Atlantic Drift</a> is thought to have at least some influence on climate. For example, the Gulf Stream helps moderate winter temperatures along the coastline of southeastern North America, keeping it warmer in winter along the coast than inland areas. The Gulf Stream also keeps extreme temperatures from occurring on the Florida Peninsula. In the higher latitudes, the North Atlantic Drift, warms the atmosphere over the oceans, keeping the British Isles and north-western Europe mild and cloudy, and not severely cold in winter like other locations at the same high latitude. The cold water currents contribute to heavy fog off the coast of eastern Canada (the <a href="/wiki/Grand_Banks_of_Newfoundland" title="Grand Banks of Newfoundland">Grand Banks of Newfoundland</a> area) and Africa\'s north-western coast.  In general, winds transport moisture and air over land areas.<sup id="cite_ref-USN-2001_5-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-USN-2001-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Natural_hazards">Natural hazards</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Atlantic_Ocean&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Natural hazards">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Iceberg_A22A,_South_Atlantic_Ocean.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Overhead photo of iceberg" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/Iceberg_A22A%2C_South_Atlantic_Ocean.jpg/220px-Iceberg_A22A%2C_South_Atlantic_Ocean.jpg" width="220" height="146" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/Iceberg_A22A%2C_South_Atlantic_Ocean.jpg/330px-Iceberg_A22A%2C_South_Atlantic_Ocean.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/Iceberg_A22A%2C_South_Atlantic_Ocean.jpg/440px-Iceberg_A22A%2C_South_Atlantic_Ocean.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2345" data-file-height="1553" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Iceberg_A22A,_South_Atlantic_Ocean.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Iceberg A22A in the South Atlantic Ocean</div></div></div>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Iceberg" title="Iceberg">Icebergs</a> are common from early February to the end of July across the shipping lanes near the <a href="/wiki/Grand_Banks_of_Newfoundland" title="Grand Banks of Newfoundland">Grand Banks of Newfoundland</a>. The ice season is longer in the polar regions, but there is little shipping in those areas.<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Hurricanes" class="mw-redirect" title="Hurricanes">Hurricanes</a> are hazard in the western parts of the North Atlantic during the summer and autumn. Due to a consistently strong <a href="/wiki/Wind_shear" title="Wind shear">wind shear</a> and a weak <a href="/wiki/Intertropical_Convergence_Zone" title="Intertropical Convergence Zone">Intertropical Convergence Zone</a>, they are practically unknown in the South Atlantic.<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Plate_tectonics">Plate tectonics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Atlantic_Ocean&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Plate tectonics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Pangea_animation_03.gif" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/8/8e/Pangea_animation_03.gif" width="200" height="160" class="thumbimage" data-file-width="200" data-file-height="160" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Pangea_animation_03.gif" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The break-up of Pangaea resulted in the opening of the Atlantic Ocean in three stages</div></div></div>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Central_Atlantic">Central Atlantic</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Atlantic_Ocean&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Central Atlantic">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r856303569">@media all and (max-width:720px){.mw-parser-output .tmulti>.thumbinner{width:100%!important;max-width:none!important}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .tsingle{float:none!important;max-width:none!important;width:100%!important;text-align:center}}</style><div class="thumb tmulti tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:184px;max-width:184px"><div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:182px;max-width:182px"><div class="thumbimage"><a href="/wiki/File:Blakey_200Ma_-_COL.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a4/Blakey_200Ma_-_COL.jpg/180px-Blakey_200Ma_-_COL.jpg" width="180" height="136" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/a/a4/Blakey_200Ma_-_COL.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="250" data-file-height="189" /></a></div></div><div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:182px;max-width:182px"><div class="thumbimage"><a href="/wiki/File:Blakey_170Ma_-_COL.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/Blakey_170Ma_-_COL.jpg/180px-Blakey_170Ma_-_COL.jpg" width="180" height="142" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/Blakey_170Ma_-_COL.jpg/270px-Blakey_170Ma_-_COL.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/b/b4/Blakey_170Ma_-_COL.jpg 2x" data-file-width="273" data-file-height="216" /></a></div></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left;text-align:left;background-color:transparent">Opening of the Central Atlantic 200-170 Ma</div></div></div>\n<p>The break-up of Pangaea began in the Central Atlantic, between North America and Northwest Africa, where rift basins opened during the Late Triassic and Early Jurassic.  This period also saw the first stages of the uplift of the Atlas Mountains.  The exact timing is controversial with estimates ranging from 200 to 170&#160;Ma.<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The opening of the Atlantic Ocean coincided with the initial break-up of the supercontinent <a href="/wiki/Pangaea" title="Pangaea">Pangaea</a>, both of which were initiated by the eruption of the <a href="/wiki/Central_Atlantic_Magmatic_Province" class="mw-redirect" title="Central Atlantic Magmatic Province">Central Atlantic Magmatic Province</a> (CAMP), one of the most extensive and voluminous <a href="/wiki/Large_igneous_province" title="Large igneous province">large igneous provinces</a> in Earth\'s history associated with the <a href="/wiki/Triassic%E2%80%93Jurassic_extinction_event" title="Triassic\xe2\x80\x93Jurassic extinction event">Triassic\xe2\x80\x93Jurassic extinction event</a>, one of Earth\'s major <a href="/wiki/Extinction_event" title="Extinction event">extinction events</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup>\nTheoliitic <a href="/wiki/Dike_(geology)" title="Dike (geology)">dikes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Aquatic_sill" title="Aquatic sill">sills</a>, and lava flows from the CAMP eruption at 200&#160;Ma have been found in West Africa, eastern North America, and northern South America.  The extent of the volcanism has been estimated to 4.5<span style="margin:0 .15em 0 .25em">\xc3\x97</span>10<sup>6</sup>&#160;km<sup>2</sup> (1.7<span style="margin:0 .15em 0 .25em">\xc3\x97</span>10<sup>6</sup>&#160;sq&#160;mi) of which 2.5<span style="margin:0 .15em 0 .25em">\xc3\x97</span>10<sup>6</sup>&#160;km<sup>2</sup> (9.7<span style="margin:0 .15em 0 .25em">\xc3\x97</span>10<sup>5</sup>&#160;sq&#160;mi) covered what is now northern and central Brazil.<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The formation of the <a href="/wiki/Central_American_Isthmus" class="mw-redirect" title="Central American Isthmus">Central American Isthmus</a> closed the <a href="/wiki/Central_American_Seaway" title="Central American Seaway">Central American Seaway</a> at the end of the Pliocene 2.8&#160;Ma ago. The formation of the isthmus resulted in the migration and extinction of many land-living animals, known as the <a href="/wiki/Great_American_Interchange" title="Great American Interchange">Great American Interchange</a>, but the closure of the seaway resulted in a "Great American Schism" as it affected ocean currents, salinity, and temperatures in both the Atlantic and Pacific.  Marine organisms on both sides of the isthmus became isolated and either diverged or went extinct.<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Northern_Atlantic"><span id="NORTH_ATLANTIC"></span><span id="Northern_Atlantic">Northern Atlantic</span></span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Atlantic_Ocean&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Northern Atlantic">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Opening_of_the_North_Atlantic_Ocean" title="Opening of the North Atlantic Ocean">Opening of the North Atlantic Ocean</a></div>\n<p>Geologically the Northern Atlantic is the area delimited to the south by two conjugate margins, Newfoundland and Iberia, and to the north by the Arctic <a href="/wiki/Eurasian_Basin" title="Eurasian Basin">Eurasian Basin</a>.  The opening of the Northern Atlantic closely followed the margins of its predecessor, the <a href="/wiki/Iapetus_Ocean" title="Iapetus Ocean">Iapetus Ocean</a>, and spread from the Central Atlantic in six stages: <a href="/wiki/Iberian_Peninsula" title="Iberian Peninsula">Iberia</a>\xe2\x80\x93<a href="/wiki/Newfoundland_(island)" title="Newfoundland (island)">Newfoundland</a>, <a href="/wiki/Porcupine_Bank" title="Porcupine Bank">Porcupine</a>\xe2\x80\x93North America, Eurasia\xe2\x80\x93Greenland, Eurasia\xe2\x80\x93North America.  Active and inactive spreading systems in this area are marked by the interaction with the <a href="/wiki/Iceland_hotspot" title="Iceland hotspot">Iceland hotspot</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="South_Atlantic">South Atlantic</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Atlantic_Ocean&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: South Atlantic">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r856303569"/><div class="thumb tmulti tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:184px;max-width:184px"><div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:182px;max-width:182px"><div class="thumbimage"><a href="/wiki/File:Gondwana115Ma_hg.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/35/Gondwana115Ma_hg.png/180px-Gondwana115Ma_hg.png" width="180" height="134" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/35/Gondwana115Ma_hg.png/270px-Gondwana115Ma_hg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/35/Gondwana115Ma_hg.png/360px-Gondwana115Ma_hg.png 2x" data-file-width="620" data-file-height="463" /></a></div></div><div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:182px;max-width:182px"><div class="thumbimage"><a href="/wiki/File:Gondwana065Ma_hg.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/Gondwana065Ma_hg.png/180px-Gondwana065Ma_hg.png" width="180" height="134" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/Gondwana065Ma_hg.png/270px-Gondwana065Ma_hg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/Gondwana065Ma_hg.png/360px-Gondwana065Ma_hg.png 2x" data-file-width="620" data-file-height="463" /></a></div></div><div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:182px;max-width:182px"><div class="thumbimage"><a href="/wiki/File:Gondwana038ma_hg.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/Gondwana038ma_hg.png/180px-Gondwana038ma_hg.png" width="180" height="134" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/Gondwana038ma_hg.png/270px-Gondwana038ma_hg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/Gondwana038ma_hg.png/360px-Gondwana038ma_hg.png 2x" data-file-width="620" data-file-height="463" /></a></div></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left;text-align:left;background-color:transparent">The opening of the South Atlantic</div></div></div>\n<p>West Gondwana (South America and Africa) broke up in the Early Cretaceous to form the South Atlantic.  The apparent fit between the coastlines of the two continents was noted on the first maps that included the South Atlantic and it was also the subject of the first computer-assisted plate tectonic reconstructions in 1965.<sup id="cite_ref-Eagles-intro_46-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Eagles-intro-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup>  This magnificent fit, however, has since then proven problematic and later reconstructions have introduced various deformation zones along the shorelines to accommodate the northward-propagating break-up.<sup id="cite_ref-Eagles-intro_46-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Eagles-intro-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup>  Intra-continental rifts and deformations have also been introduced to subdivide both continental plates into sub-plates.<sup id="cite_ref-Seton-2012-p217_48-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Seton-2012-p217-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Geologically the South Atlantic can be divided into four segments: Equatorial segment, from 10\xc2\xb0N to the Romanche Fracture Zone (RFZ);; Central segment, from RFZ to Florianopolis Fracture Zone (FFZ, north of Walvis Ridge and Rio Grande Rise); Southern segment, from FFZ to the Agulhas-Falkland Fracture Zone (AFFZ); and Falkland segment, south of AFFZ.<sup id="cite_ref-Torsvik2009-p1316_49-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Torsvik2009-p1316-49">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In the southern segment the Early Cretaceous (133\xe2\x80\x93130&#160;Ma) intensive <a href="/wiki/Magmatism" title="Magmatism">magmatism</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Paran%C3%A1_and_Etendeka_traps" title="Paran\xc3\xa1 and Etendeka traps">Paran\xc3\xa1\xe2\x80\x93Etendeka Large Igneous Province</a> produced by the <a href="/wiki/Tristan_hotspot" title="Tristan hotspot">Tristan hotspot</a> resulted in an estimated volume of 1.5<span style="margin:0 .15em 0 .25em">\xc3\x97</span>10<sup>6</sup> to 2.0<span style="margin:0 .15em 0 .25em">\xc3\x97</span>10<sup>6</sup>&#160;km<sup>3</sup> (3.6<span style="margin:0 .15em 0 .25em">\xc3\x97</span>10<sup>5</sup> to 4.8<span style="margin:0 .15em 0 .25em">\xc3\x97</span>10<sup>5</sup>&#160;cu&#160;mi). It covered an area of 1.2<span style="margin:0 .15em 0 .25em">\xc3\x97</span>10<sup>6</sup> to 1.6<span style="margin:0 .15em 0 .25em">\xc3\x97</span>10<sup>6</sup>&#160;km<sup>2</sup> (4.6<span style="margin:0 .15em 0 .25em">\xc3\x97</span>10<sup>5</sup> to 6.2<span style="margin:0 .15em 0 .25em">\xc3\x97</span>10<sup>5</sup>&#160;sq&#160;mi) in Brazil, Paraguay, and Uruguay and 0.8<span style="margin:0 .15em 0 .25em">\xc3\x97</span>10<sup>5</sup>&#160;km<sup>2</sup> (3.1<span style="margin:0 .15em 0 .25em">\xc3\x97</span>10<sup>4</sup>&#160;sq&#160;mi) in Africa. <a href="/wiki/Dyke_swarm" class="mw-redirect" title="Dyke swarm">Dyke swarms</a> in Brazil, Angola, eastern Paraguay, and Namibia, however, suggest the LIP originally covered a much larger area and also indicate failed rifts in all these areas.  Associated offshore basaltic flows reach as far south as the Falkland Islands and South Africa.  Traces of magmatism in both offshore and onshore basins in the central and southern segments have been dated to 147\xe2\x80\x9349&#160;Ma with two peaks between 143\xe2\x80\x93121&#160;Ma and 90\xe2\x80\x9360&#160;Ma.<sup id="cite_ref-Torsvik2009-p1316_49-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Torsvik2009-p1316-49">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In the Falkland segment rifting began with dextral movements between the Patagonia and Colorado sub-plates between the Early Jurassic (190&#160;Ma) and the Early Cretaceous (126.7&#160;Ma).  Around 150&#160;Ma sea-floor spreading propagated northward into the southern segment.  No later than 130&#160;Ma rifting had reached the Walvis Ridge\xe2\x80\x93Rio Grande Rise.<sup id="cite_ref-Seton-2012-p217_48-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Seton-2012-p217-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In the central segment rifting started to break Africa in two by opening the <a href="/wiki/Benue_Trough" title="Benue Trough">Benue Trough</a> around 118&#160;Ma.  Rifting in the central segment, however, coincided with the <a href="/wiki/Geomagnetic_reversal#Superchrons" title="Geomagnetic reversal">Cretaceous Normal Superchron</a> (also known as the Cretaceous quiet period), a 40&#160;Ma period without magnetic reversals, which makes it difficult to date sea-floor spreading in this segment.<sup id="cite_ref-Seton-2012-p217_48-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Seton-2012-p217-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The equatorial segment is the last phase of the break-up, but, because it is located on the Equator, magnetic anomalies cannot be used for dating.  Various estimates date the propagation of sea-floor spreading in this segment to the period 120\xe2\x80\x9396&#160;Ma.  This final stage, nevertheless, coincided with or resulted in the end of continental extension in Africa.<sup id="cite_ref-Seton-2012-p217_48-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Seton-2012-p217-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>About 50&#160;Ma the opening of the <a href="/wiki/Drake_Passage" title="Drake Passage">Drake Passage</a> resulted from a change in the motions and separation rate of the South American and Antarctic plates.
